201506	17	2015-06-17	meeting	YELLEN	And now, let me call on Simon to deliver the Desk report.	61	6
201506	18	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	61	6
201506	19	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Over the intermeeting period, the 10-year Treasury yield increased 39 basis points, moving in tandem with a more pronounced 67 basis point rise in the 10-year German yield.	61	6
201506	20	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The moves were concentrated in longer-dated forwards and occurred after German interest rates had reached historically low levels, as shown in the top-left panel of your first exhibit.	61	6
201506	21	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	This rise in nominal forward rates largely retraced the declines that began after last year’s Jackson Hole speech by ECB President Draghi and continued through the start of QE in the euro area earlier this year.	61	6
201506	22	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The size and speed of the moves in long rates were similar to the so-called taper tantrum in 2013.	61	6
201506	23	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	However, as I’ll discuss in a bit, these moves had more limited knock-on effects across financial markets.	61	6
201506	24	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Recent US data releases suggested to contacts that disappointing Q1 economic growth may have been largely due to transitory factors, but the data generally were not seen as sufficiently strong to prompt the Committee to start the normalization process at the June or July meetings.	61	6
201506	25	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	On average, buy-side and primary dealer respondents to the Desk’s June surveys assigned a near-zero probability to liftoff occurring at this meeting and reduced the odds assigned to the July meeting, as shown in the top-right panel.	61	6
201506	26	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The average probability assigned to liftoff in September or later increased for both groups.	61	6
201506	27	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The vast majority of respondents to our surveys put the probability of a liftoff in September somewhere between 30 and 70 percent.	61	6
201506	28	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Beyond liftoff, survey respondents’ modal and mean expectations for the level of the federal funds rate over the next several years were little changed.	61	6
201506	29	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The market-implied path of the federal funds rate in the very near term was also little changed over the period, though the path two to three years ahead shifted up slightly, as shown in the middle-left panel.	61	6
201506	30	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The path remains far lower and flatter than at the end of 2013 and is substantially below the median of the March SEP federal funds rate projections for 2016 and 2017.	61	6
201506	31	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Desk survey respondents expect the means of the rate projections to decline in the June SEP; they also expect the dispersion of projections across FOMC participants to narrow.	61	6
201506	32	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The upward shift in the market-implied path, alongside relatively steady survey expectations, suggests that the rise in market rates over the period was due to an increase in near-dated term premiums.	61	6
201506	33	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Indeed, the difference between the end-2017 market-implied rate and the mean PDF-implied federal funds rate expectation obtained from the Desk surveys—a measure of the term premium at this horizon— became less negative over the intermeeting period for both buy- and sell-side respondents, as shown in the middle-right panel.	61	6
201506	34	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The increase in these near-dated term premiums may stem from reduced concerns over negative shocks and from higher interest rates abroad.	61	6
201506	35	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The Desk surveys suggest that the rise in longer-dated US Treasury yields was also due to increased term premiums, as shown in the bottom-left panel.	61	6
201506	36	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	In particular, when asked to decompose the 36 basis point change in the 10-year rate between April 28 and June 3, survey respondents on average ascribed 23 basis points, or 63 percent of the move, to a rise in the term premium, which, in their qualitative comments, was linked to spillover from European markets.	61	6
201506	37	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	On average, respondents attributed 9 basis points, or one-quarter of the move, to higher expected average real policy rates.	61	6
201506	38	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	There was only a minimal attribution given to a higher expected average inflation rate.	61	6
201506	39	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The staff’s term-structure models also attribute most of the move to movements in term premiums.	61	6
201506	40	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the left half of the bottom-right panel, nearly all of the 42 basis point rise in US forward nominal rates over the period came from higher real yields, with forward measures of domestic inflation compensation relatively little changed.	61	6
201506	41	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The right half of the panel shows that real rates also accounted for most of the move in the euro-area rates.	61	6
201506	42	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Inflation compensation accounted for a slightly larger portion of the European rate moves in comparison to the United States, as recent higher euro-area realized core inflation reportedly reduced the odds some assigned to very low longer-run inflation outcomes in the region.	61	6
201506	43	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Market participants attributed the rise in euro-area real rates largely to higher term premiums.	61	6
201506	44	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Market participants highlighted a number of technical factors that likely exacerbated the rise in euro-area yields.	61	6
201506	45	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Many noted a reduction or reversal of extended long positions by momentum-driven leveraged investors, whose assets under management have grown substantially over recent years.	61	6
201506	46	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	In addition, higher euro-area interest rate volatility may have caused investors who rely on value-at-risk models to pare back their holdings.	61	6
201506	47	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The top-left panel of your next exhibit shows this volatility using the 30-day average intraday trading range on the 10-year bund yield, which rose to its highest levels in three years.	61	6
201506	48	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Over the period, Draghi noted that market participants “should get used to periods of higher volatility,” and contacts reported that this stated comfort with recent moves contributed to further upward pressure on rates.	61	6
201506	49	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	During the sharp rate moves and since the onset of the ECB’s asset purchases, anecdotal reports from Desk counterparties indicate that liquidity conditions in the euro-area fixed-income markets have deteriorated, though this is not borne out in liquidity metrics we typically monitor.	61	6
201506	50	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	However, the rise in interest rates has decreased the amount of European securities trading with negative yields and alleviated some associated challenges for investors.	61	6
201506	51	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Indeed, there are a greater number of positive-yielding securities available for reinvestment from SOMA’s maturing euro-denominated bond holdings.	61	6
201506	52	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The sharp rise in longer-term rates over the period has drawn comparisons with the taper tantrum of 2013, with some even labeling the selloff in euro-area rates as the “bund tantrum.”	61	6
201506	53	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	However, the deterioration in risk sentiment evidenced across a multitude of global risk assets has been much less acute, as shown in the top-right panel.	61	6
201506	54	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The disparate reaction of risk assets may be a result of a more limited shift in US monetary policy expectations, a smaller increase in US interest rates, and the backdrop of ECB and BOJ asset purchases continuing well into 2016.	61	6
201506	55	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	In addition, valuations and positioning were reportedly not as extended across credit and emerging market assets as we know, albeit ex post, they were in 2013, which may account for some of the differences.	61	6
201506	56	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Although the most severe risks to emerging market asset prices associated with a sharp increase in US term premiums—risks discussed in the recent QS report—have thus far not materialized, vulnerabilities remain, especially if macroeconomic fundamentals in troubled emerging markets weaken further.	61	6
201506	57	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Emerging market currencies depreciated against the dollar over the period, also shown in the middle-left panel, but to a lesser extent than following the taper tantrum.	61	6
201506	58	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	On net, the DXY dollar index was little changed, though there were significant movements in some major currency pairs.	61	6
201506	59	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The foreign exchange value of the euro continued to display very little reaction to the precarious situation in Greece, and spillover to other euro-area assets remained limited.	61	6
201506	60	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	For example, as shown in the middle-right panel, Italian spreads to German rates remained relatively stable amidst Greek bank deposit outflows, although this and other peripheral-country spreads have widened over the most recent few trading sessions.	61	6
201506	61	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Greek deposit outflows have been offset by increases in Eurosystem emergency liquidity assistance.	61	6
201506	62	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Greece’s inability to reach an agreement on a new program with its creditors could lead it not to deliver payments to the IMF at the end of the month and payments to the ECB over the following two months.	61	6
201506	63	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	A payment delay or default could, in turn, lead the ECB to cut off or severely limit ELA to Greek banks, resulting in bank holidays, capital controls, and the potential for an exit from the euro area.	61	6
201506	64	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	However, most market participants still expect Greece and its creditors to reach a resolution that would allow it to remain in the euro area.	61	6
201506	65	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Beth Anne will discuss the Greek situation further in her briefing.	61	6
201506	66	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	I should note that last Wednesday, the ECB saw a small bid at its seven-day US dollar liquidity auction, the first since mid-September 2014.	61	6
201506	67	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Outside Greece, another possible risk in markets relates to the dramatic rise in Chinese equity prices, which, despite the country’s slowing growth, increased 13 percent over the period and are nearly 150 percent higher over the past year.	61	6
201506	68	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Market participants frequently cite expectations for continued accommodative policies by the People’s Bank of China as the dominant driver of the rise in Chinese shares.	61	6
201506	69	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The timing of recent monetary policy easing actions is indicated by the yellow diamonds in the panel in the bottom-left.	61	6
201506	70	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The rally over the second quarter of this year has also overlapped with a significant drop in Chinese money market interest rates, which reduced the cost of leverage via equity margin financing, providing a tailwind to the equity market.	61	6
201506	71	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Margin trading is widely used by Chinese retail investors, and there has been a substantial increase in margin financing activity as a percent of the substantially higher market capitalization, as shown in the bottom-right panel.	61	6
201506	72	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Market participants have cited the use of margin financing as a contributor to the equity rally, which has led to a rapid expansion of earnings multiples.	61	6
201506	73	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Higher valuations and increased retail equity market leverage may increase risks associated with any future sharp—and possibly disorderly—equity market correction, especially reflecting wealth and confidence effects.	61	6
201506	74	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	That said, many have noted that the effect of any potential correction in mainland equity markets may be limited outside greater China.	61	6
201506	75	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Your next two exhibits focus on money markets and Desk operations.	61	6
201506	76	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The Desk’s ON RRP operations continued to provide a soft floor under both secured and unsecured short-term interest rates, as shown in the top-left panel of the third exhibit.	61	6
201506	77	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Relative to the prior intermeeting period, secured rates, on average, printed only modestly lower, while unsecured rates, such as the federal funds, were little changed.	61	6
201506	78	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Overall take-up in ON RRP operations was similar to previous periods and is shown in the top-right panel.	61	6
201506	79	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Reverse repos conducted with foreign official institutions—known as the “foreign RP pool”—remained around $150 billion over the intermeeting period.	61	6
201506	80	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The foreign RP pool is one possible destination for the runoff in nonoperational deposits held by foreign central banks, as discussed in a box in the Tealbook, Book B.	61	6
201506	81	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The Board of Governors continued its periodic testing of the TDF with a 14-day operation on May 21 and a 7-day operation on May 28.	61	6
201506	82	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The results were in line with the staff’s expectations, and market participants did not report any price action resulting from the testing.	61	6
201506	83	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The Desk’s announcement on May 20 of term RRP operations spanning the June quarter-end did not garner significant market attention.	61	6
201506	84	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Market participants generally suggested that, similar to the March quarter-end, the provision of “at least $200 billion” in term RRPs should leave sufficient headroom in the ON RRP over the quarter-end date.	61	6
201506	85	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	As noted in the Desk’s statement, additional details regarding the amount offered and the maximum offering rates will be announced on June 22.	61	6
201506	86	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	These proposed details are summarized in your middle-left panel, under the assumption that the Committee does not increase the target range for the federal funds rate at this meeting.	61	6
201506	87	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Unlike the $75 billion and $125 billion offerings in March, for the June quarter-end, the $200 billion available will be equally split across the two operations.	61	6
201506	88	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Based on our experience with the term RRPs over the March quarter-end, we feel this change could better balance demand across the two operations.	61	6
201506	89	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Further, the staff proposes setting the max offering rate at a smaller spread of 3 basis points to the prevailing ON RRP rate.	61	6
201506	90	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	This should help inform whether demand is driven more by the interest rate spread or the certainty of quarter-end supply.	61	6
201506	91	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Also, these operations will include our first test of a two-day term operation, which should give us information on how close a substitute a two-day term is to overnight operations.	61	6
201506	92	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Operationally, the staff is ready to implement ON RRPs and, if necessary, term RRPs and TDFs to support the IOER rate for liftoff at any time deemed appropriate by the Committee.	61	6
201506	93	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	As part of the planning process for the start of the normalization of policy, we would also like to update you on plans for the post-liftoff daily briefing process.	61	6
201506	94	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	These plans are designed to ensure the Committee receives timely updates on market conditions and is provided the opportunity to make adjustments to its policy tools, if necessary.	61	6
201506	95	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	As detailed in the memo circulated prior to the meeting and summarized in your middle-right panel, the staff proposes holding daily briefings from 2:00 to 3:00 pm for a period following liftoff.	61	6
201506	96	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	If a change to the parameters of the policy tools is needed, or if the Committee would like to hold a discussion on the configuration of the various policy tools, the daily briefing will be converted to an FOMC meeting upon notification by the FOMC Secretariat.	61	6
201506	97	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	If changes were made to the configuration of policy tools, a public announcement would be made at 4:30 pm on the day of any such decision.	61	6
201506	98	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Thomas will discuss the format of announcements about normalization tools in his briefing.	61	6
201506	99	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	As described in another memo to the Committee ahead of this meeting, the Desk also plans to extend the duration of our daily morning operations briefing to allow for broader coverage and discussion of Desk operations and related market developments.	61	6
201506	100	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	These extended morning briefings would likely continue for longer than the daily staff briefings to the Committee.	61	6
201506	101	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	I would like to highlight a couple of developments that could affect the Federal Reserve’s balance sheet.	61	6
201506	102	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	On May 6, the Treasury—as part of its quarterly refunding announcement—indicated it would institute a minimum cash balance of roughly $150 billion in order to cover, on average, one week of outflows from the Treasury General Account, known as the TGA.	61	6
201506	103	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the bottom-left panel, this would represent a material increase in the TGA balance relative to historical levels.	61	6
201506	104	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Recall that an increase in the TGA reduces reserves in the banking system dollar for dollar.	61	6
201506	105	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The Treasury did not specify how it would fund this increase, though most observers believe it will occur through higher bill issuance.	61	6
201506	106	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Increases in net bill supply have been inversely correlated with take-up in the ON RRP facility, so a higher TGA could be associated with lower ON RRP usage.	61	6
201506	107	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	However, if the Treasury exhausts its extraordinary measures to remain below the debt ceiling in the future, it will likely need to limit net new bill issuance and reduce its cash balance below the $150 billion minimum.	61	6
201506	108	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	A factor with the potential to have a significant effect on the asset side of the Fed’s balance sheet is the maturity of Treasury securities starting in early 2016, summarized in the bottom-right panel.	61	6
201506	109	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Treasury reinvestments have not been of material size for some time, in view of the maturity profile of the Treasury portfolio resulting from the MEP.	61	6
201506	110	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	This issue has become somewhat more prominent in market discussions as the expected timing of normalization has shifted out, leaving open the possibility that sizable Treasury maturities could occur while the current reinvestment policy is still in place.	61	6
201506	111	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Maturities of Treasury securities in the first quarter of 2016 will total $62 billion.	61	6
201506	112	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	In accordance with the Committee’s current reinvestment policy, the Desk would reinvest maturing Treasuries in new securities issued via auctions that settle on the day the maturing funds are received.	61	6
201506	113	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Specifically, as summarized in the top-left panel of your final exhibit, according to this policy, the principal amount received from maturing securities is reinvested on a noncompetitive basis and allocated in proportion to the issue sizes of all qualifying new securities.	61	6
201506	114	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	With regard to agency securities, the Desk continues to reinvest principal payments on holdings of agency debt and MBS into agency MBS through secondary market purchases, which continue to go smoothly.	61	6
201506	115	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Finally, we’d like to update you on the proposed changes to the calculation of the effective federal funds rate.	61	6
201506	116	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The Desk is proposing to change the calculation methodology to a volume-weighted median concurrent with the data-source cutover to the FR 2420 in the first part of 2016.	61	6
201506	117	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The same calculation methodology would be used for the new overnight bank funding rate.	61	6
201506	118	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Recall, at the most recent meeting, we discussed the advantages of moving to a volume-weighted median—namely, the improvement in the representativeness and robustness of the effective rate.	61	6
201506	119	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	The detailed case for making these changes was summarized in both a memo to the Committee and a technical memo to research directors.	61	6
201506	120	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	As noted in a memo sent to the FOMC last week, if the Committee is comfortable with the change to a volume-weighted median, our proposed plan to communicate these changes, outlined in your top-right panel, would be to include a summary of the proposed change and the associated discussion in the June meeting minutes as well as to release a Desk statement and a technical note with data and analysis supporting the change on the New York Fed website shortly thereafter.	61	6
201506	121	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	This would serve to enhance the public’s understanding of the rationale for transitioning to the FR 2420 data collection and changing the calculation methodology.	61	6
201506	122	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	We will plan to return to the Committee later this year to outline specific implementation features, such as a detailed timeline, a list of additional disclosures, and an overview of practices to align with IOSCO principles for financial benchmarks.	61	6
201506	123	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	61	6
201506	124	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	That concludes my prepared remarks.	61	6

201506	128	2015-06-17	meeting	ROSENGREN	Your chart 18 on the SOMA Treasury security maturities is interesting.	62	6
201506	129	2015-06-17	meeting	ROSENGREN	And so, your philosophy on duration as more and more of these securities are coming due—what is the conscious decision for the duration we’re aiming at for the overall SOMA portfolio?	62	6
201506	130	2015-06-17	meeting	ROSENGREN	Is there a target that we’re hitting?	62	6
201506	131	2015-06-17	meeting	ROSENGREN	We haven’t had much of a discussion of the duration of our portfolio, and I’m wondering whether at some point it might be worthwhile discussing.	62	6
201506	132	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	When we do rollovers, it really depends on Treasury issuance.	62	6
201506	133	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	We roll over into everything they issue except bills.	62	6
201506	134	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	So we would be adding securities to the portfolio of between 60 and 70 months, depending on what Treasury does.	62	6
201506	135	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	They might move up above 70, depending on how they assess their split between bills and notes and other issues that they have.	62	6
201506	136	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	But at the point when we’re reinvesting, we are not actively targeting any duration limit from the Desk.	62	6
201506	137	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	We’re basically just accepting what the Treasury puts out in terms of its duration.	62	6
201506	138	2015-06-17	meeting	ROSENGREN	So the debt management strategy of the Treasury is determining the duration of our portfolio going forward?	62	6
201506	139	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	No, it’s determining the duration of what we reinvest in.	62	6
201506	140	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Because we have a very large amount of Treasuries right now, it won’t have a material effect on the duration of the portfolio in 2016, if we continue doing reinvestments.	62	6
201506	141	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Remember that what we are reinvesting has also got zero duration at that point.	62	6
201506	142	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	So reinvestment will then move up the duration in a mechanical sense, but it’s not as if that’s going to affect the previous thought that we had of holding long-duration assets.	62	6
201506	143	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	That is still there.	62	6
201506	144	2015-06-17	meeting	ROSENGREN	Thank you.	62	6

201506	147	2015-06-17	meeting	DUDLEY	I just want to clarify.	62	6
201506	148	2015-06-17	meeting	DUDLEY	On panel 17, the Treasury increases its cash balance and that increases the supply of risk-free assets and reduces the demand for overnight RRP.	62	6
201506	149	2015-06-17	meeting	DUDLEY	If the debt limit becomes binding, then Treasury would draw down its cash balance.	62	6
201506	150	2015-06-17	meeting	DUDLEY	And so the demand for our overnight RRP could go up at that point in time.	62	6
201506	151	2015-06-17	meeting	DUDLEY	Is that a reasonable inference?	62	6
201506	152	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	I think there is definitely that direct effect.	62	6
201506	153	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	And we know that, if this was happening in 2016, there are lots of other changes in money markets.	62	6
201506	154	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	There’s another issue that we haven’t really faced yet.	62	6
201506	155	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Treasury bills are viewed as very safe assets, and there’s only one exception to that:  when Congress and the Treasury can’t really agree on whether to raise the debt limit.	62	6
201506	156	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	And then you could see other events happen.	62	6
201506	157	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	We saw this in 2013—a little bit of aversion to Treasury bills that would mature in the so-called red zone.	62	6
201506	158	2015-06-17	meeting	DUDLEY	And the overnight RRP might be more attractive.	62	6
201506	159	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	It’s possible.	62	6
201506	160	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	Again, if you look at the holders of Treasury bills, a large amount of the holders are foreign central banks.	62	6
201506	161	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	So it would depend on their behavior.	62	6
201506	162	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	They also have the foreign pool as another option at that point.	62	6
201506	163	2015-06-17	meeting	DUDLEY	What I’m trying to drive at is, it seems like it creates a little bit more uncertainty about what the demand for the overnight RRP would be as you go through this period.	62	6
201506	164	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	There is definitely uncertainty on the operations side for us, always, when the debt limit comes up.	62	6
201506	165	2015-06-17	meeting	POTTER	I think the larger uncertainty is going through the debt limit again and what that does for views of US policymaking and the Treasury market in general.	62	6
201506	166	2015-06-17	meeting	DUDLEY	Sure, okay.	62	6

201506	179	2015-06-17	meeting	YELLEN	For our next topic, let me call on Susan McLaughlin, who is going to discuss the Federal Reserve Bank of New York’s counterparty framework review.	96	9
201506	180	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201506	181	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	I’ll be referring to the exhibit titled “Desk Counterparty Framework.”	96	9
201506	182	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	Over the past year and a half, Federal Reserve Bank of New York and Board staff members have been engaged in a review of the Desk’s counterparty framework across all of the Desk’s operations in US and foreign financial markets.	96	9
201506	183	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	In the past, such reviews tended to occur separately for domestic and foreign operations.	96	9
201506	184	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	This is the first time the staff have reviewed the frameworks on a comprehensive basis rather than an operation-specific one.	96	9
201506	185	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	The staff’s recommendation is that such a review be conducted every three years from now on, in order to think less reactively and more strategically about how to manage Desk counterparties.	96	9
201506	186	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	The staff would consult with the Committee after each of these reviews.	96	9
201506	187	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	As noted in the memo that you received, a key theme of the review was that financial markets are evolving in response to technological and regulatory developments, and that the structure of the markets in which the Desk operates could change in ways that may benefit from a larger and/or more diverse set of counterparties in the longer term.	96	9
201506	188	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	It is too early at this point to forecast how the structure of these markets will evolve or what changes to the Desk’s counterparty framework will be required to adapt to these changes.	96	9
201506	189	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	But the staff’s review also suggested a number of enhancements that could be made in the near term to strengthen and harmonize the management of counterparties across all of the Desk’s operations.	96	9
201506	190	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	Many of the recommendations concern ways to harmonize and refine internal administration of counterparty relationships across all Desk operations and are already under way.	96	9
201506	191	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	These changes are listed in the third paragraph of exhibit 1.	96	9
201506	192	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	Additionally, the staff’s review raised two policy questions that represent near- term opportunities for additional changes to the Desk’s counterparty practices.	96	9
201506	193	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	These questions are shown in the fourth paragraph of exhibit 1.	96	9
201506	194	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	One question is whether to consider publishing lists of counterparties for FX and/or foreign reserves management operations, as is done currently for domestic OMO counterparties.	96	9
201506	195	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	As you can see in panel 2 of exhibit 2, there are currently some inconsistencies in the practices across counterparty types.	96	9
201506	196	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	While most central banks do not make information about their FX or foreign reserves management counterparties public, the Federal Reserve already discloses some of this information through the quarterly post-trade disclosures under Dodd- Frank, just as is done for OMO counterparties.	96	9
201506	197	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	However, while the lists of primary dealers and expanded reverse repo counterparties are public, the Desk does not currently publish a list of FX or foreign reserves management counterparties.	96	9
201506	198	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	Publishing counterparty lists for foreign operations would increase transparency by informing the public of all of the firms eligible for these operations and not just those firms the Desk has dealt with in a given quarter.	96	9
201506	199	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	However, publishing a list could also create the perception of a Federal Reserve imprimatur with regard to the firms listed, as seems to have occurred in the case of primary dealers.	96	9
201506	200	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	This imprimatur carries costs as well as benefits in managing these relationships.	96	9
201506	201	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	For open market operations, the benefit of publication clearly outweighs the cost.	96	9
201506	202	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	The Desk relies on primary dealers for a variety of services beyond trade execution in support of its mandate, such as FR 2004 reporting, TMPG best practice adherence, and survey responses about policy expectations before each meeting.	96	9
201506	203	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	Primary dealers are willing to serve these needs because they value the status they obtain from being listed as a primary dealer.	96	9
201506	204	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	However, the Federal Reserve plays a more limited role in the foreign financial markets in which the Desk operates and does not require the same range of services from its counterparties in those markets.	96	9
201506	205	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	So the case for providing the same type of public recognition to these counterparties through publication of a list may not exist or may be stronger, perhaps, for policy operations like FX intervention than for portfolio maintenance activities such as reinvestment of Eurobond proceeds.	96	9
201506	206	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	A second question is whether there is value to be gained by revising the eligibility requirements for primary dealers to allow for a modest expansion of the list.	96	9
201506	207	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	As is shown in panel 3 of exhibit 2, the number of primary dealers has risen modestly since the recent financial crisis but is still near the low end of the historical range.	96	9
201506	208	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	The staff believes that modifying the eligibility requirements to allow a wider range of broker- dealers and banks that make markets in government securities to be considered for primary dealer status is worthwhile, as it can potentially increase operational coverage and enhance trade execution.	96	9
201506	209	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	It will also increase coverage for Treasury auctions.	96	9
201506	210	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	Additionally, the staff views a modest expansion of the primary dealer list as an informative and low-cost initial step toward the broader counterparty framework that might be required in the future as a result of changes in market structure.	96	9
201506	211	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	It would allow the staff to build on the learning to date from the Treasury and MBS pilots the Desk has recently conducted with very small firms and could be accomplished within the scope of the existing technology infrastructure.	96	9
201506	212	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	The staff would like to pursue further work on these two questions and develop proposals to bring to the Committee later this year, along with drafts of the revised counterparty policies that would reflect the proposed changes.	96	9
201506	213	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	A tentative plan would be to publish revised counterparty policies in tandem with the announcement of the conclusion of the mortgage operations pilot shortly after year-end.	96	9
201506	214	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	The staff would greatly appreciate any feedback you may have, either now or in the coming weeks, on the questions outlined at the bottom of exhibit 1.	96	9
201506	215	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	In particular, do you agree that the staff should develop recommendations on these two issues for the Committee’s consideration later this year?	96	9
201506	216	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	Do you have reactions to the questions we have laid out?	96	9
201506	217	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	Are there other issues that you would like the staff to consider?	96	9
201506	218	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	Are you comfortable with the general timeline and approach laid out for communicating to the public in the event that the staff’s recommendations are adopted?	96	9
201506	219	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201506	220	2015-06-17	meeting	McLAUGHLIN	That concludes my prepared remarks.	96	9
201506	221	2015-06-17	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	96	9

201506	369	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	As you know, the recent spending data have been disappointing, on the whole.	11	1
201506	370	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	Indeed, as shown on line 1 of panel 1, after taking on board the indicators that became available after we closed the Tealbook projection last week, we now estimate that real GDP contracted at an annual rate of ¼ percent in the first quarter, with net exports, line 5, subtracting 1¾ percentage points off growth.	11	1
201506	371	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	Private domestic final purchases, displayed on line 3, also softened noticeably, with weaker readings for both consumption and business investment.	11	1
201506	372	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	As outlined in panel 2, a key challenge has been to assess how much of the first- quarter weakness should be carried forward.	11	1
201506	373	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	As discussed in a Tealbook box, we now judge that some of the softness reflects residual seasonality—the magnitude of which is subject to considerable uncertainty—where we expect the low reading in the first quarter will be offset by strong readings in subsequent quarters, primarily the second quarter.	11	1
201506	374	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	In addition, we suspect similar transitory effects from the harsh winter weather and the disruption of supply chains from the labor disputes at West Coast ports.	11	1
201506	375	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	But some of the weakness in Q1 appears to reflect more persistent factors.	11	1
201506	376	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	The appreciation of the dollar has imposed a substantial drag on net exports over the first half of the year, and we expect more of the same through next year.	11	1
201506	377	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	In addition, the negative effects of the drop in oil prices on drilling have left a deep imprint on investment during the first half of the year.	11	1
201506	378	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	The black line in panel 3 indicates that business fixed investment fell in the first quarter, reflecting a plunge in drilling and mining investment, shown by the blue portion of the bars—and another enormous drop in drilling appears to be underway this quarter.	11	1
201506	379	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	As shown in panel 4, the combined effects of the strong dollar and the slump in oil prices resulted in declines in manufacturing output, the red line, and total industrial production, the black line, in the first quarter, and the data in hand indicate further weakness in the current quarter.	11	1
201506	380	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	Finally, disappointing readings on personal consumption, despite the boost to purchasing power from the low oil prices, have led us to assume less momentum in consumption over the remainder of the year.	11	1
201506	381	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	So we see a wide range of evidence that there has been some deceleration in demand, but there are also indicators that the GDP data exaggerate the magnitude of that deceleration.	11	1
201506	382	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	For one, payroll employment growth, shown in panel 5, has been well maintained, albeit at a slower pace than during 2014.	11	1
201506	383	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	In addition, gross domestic income increased at a moderate rate in the first quarter, and surveys of manufacturing and nonmanufacturing purchasing managers are more upbeat than would be consistent with the GDP data.	11	1
201506	384	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	Putting all of this together, we see a somewhat weaker trajectory for real GDP growth in 2015 than we had written down in the March Tealbook.	11	1
201506	385	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	For the current quarter, we estimate that real GDP will increase 2¾ percent and then ease a bit to a 1¾ percent gain in the third quarter.	11	1
201506	386	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	As shown on line 1 in panel 6, the Blue Chip forecasters seem to assess the Q1 weakness much as we do:  Since March, they have downgraded their estimate for the first quarter, but they continue to anticipate above-trend growth in the second quarter and see average growth over the first half as likely to be considerably weaker than they were expecting three months ago.	11	1
201506	387	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	As shown on line 5, the median nowcast across the Federal Reserve System also falls in this middle ground—better than in the first quarter, but not recovering the ground that was lost then.	11	1
201506	388	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	Your next exhibit reviews our medium-term outlook.	11	1
201506	389	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	As indicated by the black line in panel 1, we project real GDP to increase at a moderate rate throughout the medium term, a pace that is little changed relative to the most recent couple of Tealbooks.	11	1
201506	390	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	The red line provides a comparison over a longer time period—namely, one year ago—and as you can see, the June 2014 Tealbook forecast for GDP growth was visibly stronger than the current projection.	11	1
201506	391	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	As noted in panel 2, the downward revision since last year is largely the result of the surprising appreciation of the dollar as well as a somewhat softer foreign outlook, topics which will be explored by Beth Anne.	11	1
201506	392	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	The drag generated by these two factors has been offset to a small extent by the effects of lower paths for interest rates, shown in panel 3, and the drop in oil prices.	11	1
201506	393	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	Compared with outside forecasters, the Tealbook has a lower path for real GDP than either the Blue Chip consensus or the Survey of Professional Forecasters, owing to more drag coming from net exports in our projection.	11	1
201506	394	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	Panel 4 examines the sources of growth of aggregate demand in the staff projection.	11	1
201506	395	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	The left bar displays the average contribution to GDP growth by sector over the past five years.	11	1
201506	396	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	For example, the blue portion of the bar indicates that rising consumption contributed about 1¾ percentage points to aggregate demand growth, on average, and business investment, the red portion of the bar, contributed nearly ¾ percentage point per year.	11	1
201506	397	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	In 2015, business investment is expected to be quite weak, owing to the plunge in drilling and mining.	11	1
201506	398	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	The orange portion of the bars displays the substantial drag arising from net exports that we anticipate this year and next, owing to the appreciation of the dollar and lackluster foreign growth.	11	1
201506	399	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	Personal consumption is the main driver of aggregate demand this year; by comparing the height of the blue bars to the black line, you can see that consumption accounts for more than all of GDP growth this year and substantial portions in 2016 and 2017.	11	1
201506	400	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	The robust increases in consumption reflect strong gains in real income, owing in part to the drop in oil prices, as well as the boost to spending from the elevated wealth-to-income ratio displayed in panel 5.	11	1
201506	401	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	One implication of the dissonance between recent data on aggregate demand and the more upbeat labor market data noted earlier is a decline in business-sector productivity, shown by the black line in panel 6.	11	1

201506	422	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	These responses are also consistent with work by the staff that indicates that the linkage between nominal wage growth and price inflation is loose.	11	1

201506	440	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Thank you, Glenn.	11	1
201506	441	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	When I queried colleagues about what they would most like in a chart show, they said “brevity,” which, unfortunately, will preclude me from delivering my stirring rendition of “American Pie: The Quarter Net Exports Died.”	11	1
201506	442	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	[Laughter]	11	1
201506	443	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	That said, I will begin with a riff on trade.	11	1
201506	444	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	As discussed by Glenn, net exports (line 1 in the table) subtracted almost 2 percentage points from first-quarter GDP growth.	11	1
201506	445	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Some of this drag reflects temporary factors— importantly, port disruptions and residual seasonality.	11	1
201506	446	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Data on shipping containers processed at West Coast ports (panel 2) indicate that disruptions to inbound containers (the red line) in January and February were made up in March, leaving little net imprint on first-quarter imports.	11	1
201506	447	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	But the recovery of outbound containers (the blue line) looks incomplete, leaving Q1 exports low, on average, and likely boosting this quarter’s figure.	11	1
201506	448	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	We have also found evidence of residual seasonality in exports for Q1 but not for imports, which further supports a payback in the current quarter.	11	1
201506	449	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Thereafter, we have growth in exports and imports largely returning to our models, which see a net exports contributing a drag on GDP growth of roughly ¾ percentage point in the second half of this year and next year, similar to that in the April Tealbook.	11	1
201506	450	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	More generally, since last year, net exports have become a major headwind to the US recovery, as can be seen by comparing the red and black lines in panel 3.	11	1
201506	451	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	This primarily reflects the unexpected strength of the broad real dollar (panel 4)—which, in contrast to the steady depreciation we had projected last June, has risen 10 percent, reflecting the relative strength of the US economy and expectations that we will be normalizing monetary policy before other major central banks.	11	1
201506	452	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Consequently, we have the dollar climbing a bit further this year, under the assumption that we are the liftoff leaders, before resuming a trajectory of slight depreciation.	11	1
201506	453	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	The effect of the dollar’s appreciation on the contribution of net exports to GDP growth is shown in panel 5.	11	1
201506	454	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	As the contour of the black line reveals, we now think the dollar’s appreciation since last summer began to depress net exports strongly last quarter, and we see the effect intensifying throughout this year before waning only slowly over the course of the next two years.	11	1
201506	455	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	The dollar is not the only factor weighing on net exports.	11	1
201506	456	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Looking at panel 6, the contribution of net exports to output growth has been lessened by a downshift in our path for foreign growth since this time last year, which I discuss in your next exhibit.	11	1
201506	457	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	The black line in panel 1 presents our current path for foreign growth, measured on a four-quarter basis to smooth out those pesky first-quarter dips.	11	1
201506	458	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	After slowing over the past year, foreign growth is expected to achieve a 3 percent pace by the middle of next year, noticeably weaker than what we had projected last year.	11	1
201506	459	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	This revision primarily reflects a step-down in our projection for the emerging market economies—panel 2—in which, instead of rising by late last year, growth fell further.	11	1
201506	460	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Undaunted, we have EME growth climbing to nearly 4 percent by the end of 2016, but this is almost ½ percentage point below what we had last June.	11	1
201506	461	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	I would note that, although we have taken down our projections for China and emerging Asia some, this revision is really a story about Latin America “losing its mojo.”	11	1
201506	462	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Falling commodity prices have laid bare structural and political weaknesses in a number of economies—importantly, Brazil—and reduced the payoff to energy reforms in Mexico.	11	1
201506	463	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	We also weakened growth in the advanced foreign economies—panel 3—in which the bounceback from Japan’s consumption tax has underwhelmed and falling oil prices have sapped Canada’s strength.	11	1
201506	464	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	But the AFE forecast is little altered beyond the near term.	11	1
201506	465	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	In general, highly accommodative monetary policy, healing credit markets, and—for some countries—lower oil prices and depreciated currencies are all supportive of growth.	11	1
201506	466	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	In particular, the euro area has been an uncharacteristic source of strength.	11	1
201506	467	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	We have growth continuing to firm and, as indicated in panel 4, we take comfort from the return to credit growth (the bars) and the steady decline in the unemployment rate (the black line).	11	1
201506	468	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	That said, as discussed in panel 5, one threat to our forecast comes from current euro-area member, Greece.	11	1
201506	469	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	As Simon discussed, Greece and its official-sector creditors are once again dancing perilously close to a default cliff, as negotiations to unlock financing to get Greece through the summer broke down over the weekend.	11	1
201506	470	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	If an agreement is not cobbled together soon, there is a significant risk that on June 30 Greece will be unable to make its payment to the IMF and its current EU aid program will expire.	11	1
201506	471	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	In this event, conditions could deteriorate quickly, with deposit flight and possibly reduced ECB liquidity provision forcing capital controls, bank holidays, and the potential for Greek exit.	11	1
201506	472	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Knowing what will happen is probably best left to oracles.	11	1
201506	473	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	For our part, we do not rule out a significant worsening in the situation but assume that the fallout is limited by the lack of financial market exposure and by euro-area firewalls.	11	1
201506	474	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Consequently, though we have built in a small drag to euro-area growth stemming from Greece, our baseline is one in which Greek developments do not derail the euro area or the global recovery.	11	1
201506	475	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	We are not naïve, however, to the significant risks that attend this forecast.	11	1
201506	476	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	I have two additional observations.	11	1
201506	477	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	First, as evidenced by panel 6, market response, though negative, is still well below what we have seen in earlier high-drama Greek moments.	11	1
201506	478	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	What is critically different now is that the establishment of the ECB bond-buying programs, EU financial backstops, and progress on banking union have helped to delink fears of Greek exit from that of full euro-area breakup and, thus, better positioned Europe and the global economy to endure a “Greek tragedy.”	11	1
201506	479	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Ironically, however, by reducing the sense of urgency concerning a Greek exit, these institutional improvements may have actually increased the risk of it happening.	11	1
201506	480	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Second, although all attention is now focused on a short-term agreement to get Greece through the summer, this will not solve the underlying problems.	11	1
201506	481	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	To put Greece on a sustainable path while remaining within the euro area, the Greek government will have to deliver a fundamental reform of their economy and other euro-area members will have to deliver significant further funding.	11	1
201506	482	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Shaking off Greece blues and turning to exhibit 7, our projection of foreign growth settling in at a moderate, near-potential pace by the end of our forecast period is associated with a return to modest inflation (panel 1) in both the AFEs and EMEs, as oil and nonfuel commodity prices (panel 2) stabilize at low levels.	11	1
201506	483	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Against this background, we assume monetary policy remains quite accommodative, as policy rates (panel 3) remain low and central bank balance sheets (panel 4) are still elevated.	11	1
201506	484	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	I should note that, as seen in panel 5, the jump in long-term yields witnessed over the intermeeting period, although sharp, still leaves 10-year rates at very low levels.	11	1
201506	485	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	A key question going forward, and one at the heart of the secular stagnation debate, is whether the current depressed level of interest rates is indicative of a permanent downshift in rates or whether, as growth firms, interest rates will rise closer to pre-crises levels.	11	1
201506	486	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Turning to exhibit 8, to look at this question for the AFEs, I examine one important determinant of the level of interest rates:  the equilibrium interest rate, or the short-term real interest rate consistent with output at potential and stable inflation.	11	1
201506	487	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Here, I present estimates of the equilibrium rate in the AFE economies using two approaches, outlined in panel 1 and discussed in more detail in a box by Andrea Raffo in the international section of Tealbook, Book A.	11	1
201506	488	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	The first approach focuses on identifying cyclical movements of the short-run equilibrium rate around its steady- state value, which is assumed to be constant over time.	11	1
201506	489	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	It uses an estimated dynamic stochastic general equilibrium, or DSGE, model consisting of a Phillips curve equation, an IS equation, and a Taylor rule.	11	1
201506	490	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Panel 2 shows the evolution of the AFE cyclical aggregate equilibrium interest rate based on this approach.	11	1
201506	491	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	According to the model, the AFE equilibrium real interest rate plunged in the aftermath of the global financial crisis and currently stands in negative territory, about 3½ percentage points below its steady-state value of nearly 2 percent.	11	1
201506	492	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Moreover, the effects of the recession are very persistent:  Absent additional shocks, the AFE aggregate equilibrium interest rate by 2020 is projected to still be more than ½ percentage point below steady state.	11	1
201506	493	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Thus, viewed within this framework, the current low level of interest rates does not necessarily imply a lower long-run level of the equilibrium rate; the current level could rather be consistent with slow adjustment of rates to the steady state.	11	1
201506	494	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Other evidence, however, including that based on the framework in Laubach and Williams (2003), suggests that the long-run value of the equilibrium interest rate in the United States may drift over time.	11	1
201506	495	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Their approach starts from an empirical framework that includes an IS equation and a Phillips curve equation and focuses on estimating the link between slow-moving changes in the equilibrium interest rate and in trend growth of output.	11	1
201506	496	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Should you have any questions on this approach, you should feel free to bring it up with them directly.	11	1
201506	497	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Their machinery generates this result that, indeed, the long-run equilibrium rate in the United States, shown by the black line in panel 3, has declined significantly over the past decade.	11	1
201506	498	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Applying a similar methodology to data for the AFEs, we find that the average AFE equilibrium rate (the red line) has also fallen steadily and is currently negative.	11	1
201506	499	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	This result suggests that AFE policy rates may remain much lower than in the early 2000s for structural, rather than cyclical, reasons.	11	1
201506	500	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Should this be the case, AFE policymakers may find it more difficult to face future recessions using only conventional tools.	11	1
201506	501	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	However, one should be cautious about inferring that the equilibrium interest rate is permanently lower.	11	1
201506	502	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	These estimates are very imprecise, and the underlying determinants of the equilibrium rate can change over time.	11	1
201506	503	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Some factors that have been cited as likely pushing down the equilibrium rate, such as declining working-age population, seem hard to change, but other factors, particularly relevant to open economies, may give cause for hope.	11	1
201506	504	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	For example, some of the decline in equilibrium rates has been attributed to the global savings glut—the phenomenon that, before the global financial crisis, especially in the mid-2000s, countries with large current account surpluses (the red bars in panel 4) were exporting their savings to advanced economies, particularly the United States, which ran current account deficits.	11	1
201506	505	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	For the period ahead, we anticipate that the negative pressure on rates stemming from these flows will ease from its peak, as the current account surpluses of the oil exporters narrow and the Asian economies rebalance some toward domestic demand.	11	1
201506	506	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Finally, in an open economy setting, equilibrium interest rates in the advanced economies—importantly, the United States—may be affected not just by domestic potential output growth, but also by equilibrium interest rates and potential growth in the rest of the world.	11	1
201506	507	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	In contrast to the United States and the AFEs, world potential growth (the black line in panel 5) has been stable, as the share of world GDP represented by the faster-growing EMEs is rising over time, which may argue for a higher steady-state value of the equilibrium rate than if only domestic potential growth mattered.	11	1
201506	508	2015-06-17	meeting	WILSON	Thank you.	11	1
201506	509	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	I will be referring to the packet labeled “Materials for Briefing on the Summary of Economic Projections.”	11	1
201506	510	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Exhibit 1 shows the trajectories of your economic projections conditional on your individual assessments of appropriate monetary policy.	11	1
201506	511	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	As illustrated by the top panel, most of you project that real GDP growth this year will average somewhat less than its longer-run pace.	11	1
201506	512	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	All of you see GDP accelerating appreciably in the second half of 2015, and project that growth will exceed its longer-run pace in 2016.	11	1
201506	513	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	For 2017, nearly all of you see real GDP either growing at, or decelerating toward, its longer-run rate.	11	1
201506	514	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	All of you project some further reduction in the unemployment rate by the end of next year; indeed, a sizable majority of you see the unemployment rate within 0.2 percentage point of its longer- run normal value in late 2016.	11	1
201506	515	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	In addition, about half of you project that at the end of 2017, the unemployment rate will be below your estimate of its longer-run normal rate.	11	1
201506	516	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	As shown in the third panel, headline PCE inflation is expected to come in at or below 1 percent this year, but to climb to 1½ percent or more in 2016.	11	1
201506	517	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Even so, nearly half of you anticipate that headline inflation next year will run more than ¼ percentage point below your longer-run objective.	11	1
201506	518	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	In contrast, a sizable majority of you project that headline inflation will be within 0.1 percentage point of the Committee’s goal in 2017.	11	1
201506	519	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	The final panel shows that you project only a slight decline in core PCE inflation this year and that you expect a gradual rise over the remainder of the forecast.	11	1
201506	520	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Exhibit 2 compares your current projections with those in the March Summary of Economic Projections and with the June Tealbook.	11	1
201506	521	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	As indicated in the top panel, you responded to the evidence of weak economic activity early in the year by marking down significantly your forecasts of real GDP growth in 2015; however, most of you ascribed the first-half weakness in activity largely to transitory factors, and so your medium- and longer-term projections differ only a little from what you wrote down in March.	11	1
201506	522	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	As shown in the second panel, the central tendency of your forecasts for the unemployment rate edged up 0.1 percentage point or so in 2015, with a number of you attributing the revision to the weaker-than-expected first-half data.	11	1
201506	523	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	According to several of you, the scant differences between your March and June projections of GDP growth and the unemployment rate in 2016 and 2017, despite recent indications of a weaker near-term trajectory for expenditures, is due in part to the monetary policy response to that weakness that you incorporated in your forecasts.	11	1
201506	524	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	As the bottom panels indicate, your projections of both headline and core PCE inflation for all three years were very similar to what you wrote down in March.	11	1
201506	525	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	The revisions you made to your forecasts for real GDP growth in 2015—which reduce the midpoint of the central tendency by 0.6 percentage point—were a bit larger than the change in the staff outlook.	11	1
201506	526	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	As a consequence, the Tealbook forecast for economic growth in 2015 remains below your central tendency, but by a smaller margin than was the case in March.	11	1
201506	527	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Otherwise, the Tealbook forecasts for economic growth and inflation continue to run near the bottom of your central tendencies over the projection period, and the staff’s projections of the unemployment rate remain at about the upper end of your central tendencies.	11	1
201506	528	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Exhibit 3 summarizes your assessments of the quarter in which you judge that the first increase in the target range for the federal funds rate will be appropriate, along with the economic conditions you anticipate at that time.	11	1
201506	529	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	As shown in the top panel, a majority of you currently view the third quarter of 2015 as likely to be the appropriate time to commence tightening.	11	1
201506	530	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Since March, nine of you pushed back your prescribed date of departure from the effective lower bound by one quarter.	11	1
201506	531	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	As indicated by the panels at the bottom, your current projections of the unemployment rate and for core inflation at the time of initial tightening show a bit more dispersion in this forecast than in March; however, the medians of the unemployment rate, at 5.3 percent, and of core inflation, at 1.2 percent, are unchanged from before.	11	1
201506	532	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	All but four of you project that the unemployment rate at the time of the first increase in the federal funds rate will still be above its longer-run normal level, and all but one of you project that core inflation will be well below the Committee’s longer-run objective for headline inflation of 2 percent.	11	1
201506	533	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Nonetheless, all of you project further progress toward the Committee’s objectives after departure from the effective lower bound.	11	1
201506	534	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Exhibit 4 provides an overview of your assessments of the appropriate path for the federal funds rate at the end of each year of the forecast period and over the longer run.	11	1
201506	535	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Most of you now consider a lower federal funds rate than you had projected in March to be appropriate over some part of the projection period; how you implemented this view varied from person to person.	11	1
201506	536	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Nine of you now judge that the outlook warrants a somewhat later date of initial tightening, although not all of the 9 see this as justifying a lower federal funds rate at the end of this year.	11	1
201506	537	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	All told, 10 participants thought that the current outlook warranted a lower federal funds rate at the end of 2015, of which 8 thought this easier stance of policy should continue at least until the end of 2016.	11	1
201506	538	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Two of you indicated that modestly lower estimates of the longer-run level of the real interest rate also played a role in reducing the path for the nominal federal funds rate.	11	1
201506	539	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	On the numbers, your mean federal funds rate projection for 2015 has come down 19 basis points to 0.58 percent, although the median federal funds rate projection for that date is unchanged from March at 0.63 percent.	11	1
201506	540	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	The median projections for the ends of 2016 and 2017 are 1.63 and 2.88 percent, respectively; both are down 25 basis points from what they were in March.	11	1
201506	541	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Exhibit 5 shows your assessments of the uncertainty and risks surrounding your economic projections.	11	1
201506	542	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	As shown in the figures to the left, your views regarding uncertainty have not changed in a material way.	11	1
201506	543	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	A sizable majority of you judge the level of uncertainty about your individual projections of GDP growth and the unemployment rate to be broadly similar to the average level over the past 20 years, with only slightly less agreement on headline and core inflation.	11	1
201506	544	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	The panels to the right indicate that a large majority also continues to see the risks to real GDP growth and the unemployment rate as broadly balanced.	11	1
201506	545	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	As reported in the third and fourth panels to the right, 11 of you now see risks to headline and core inflation as balanced—3 more than was the case in March.	11	1
201506	546	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	A few of you have indicated that your confidence in the likelihood has increased of inflation moving toward the policy objective of a 2 percent rate.	11	1
201506	547	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Finally, exhibit 6 takes a closer look at recent assessments you have made of the appropriate path for the federal funds rate.	11	1
201506	548	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Unlike in exhibit 4, the blue dots in the top panel of this exhibit show your projections for the federal funds rate for the end of a single year—2016—but for different forecast vintages, from September 2013, the first SEP in which you supplied a forecast for 2016, to this meeting.	11	1
201506	549	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	The blue line connects the median values of your federal funds rate projections for these eight forecasts.	11	1
201506	550	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	As you can see, the median of your views with regard to the appropriate federal funds rate has varied over time.	11	1
201506	551	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	In particular, from the low level recorded in the December 2013 SEP, the median climbed more than 100 basis points by September 2014, but then more than retraced that increase to reach its nadir with this projection.	11	1
201506	552	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Building on some analysis that was presented to you at the March meeting, I look at your policy prescriptions through the lens of a policy rule—in particular, the noninertial Taylor (1999) rule, shown in the middle panel.	11	1
201506	553	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	As in the staff’s SEP briefing in March, I use your individual SEP submissions for 2016 as the right-hand- side variables in the Taylor rule equation, while also employing your estimates of the longer-run normal level of the unemployment rate and the longer-run real federal funds rate.	11	1
201506	554	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	The median of those 17 estimates at each forecast date is plotted as the solid red line back in the panel at the top.	11	1
201506	555	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Three points emerge from this exercise.	11	1
201506	556	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	First, the median Taylor-rule-implied policy rates for 2016 have been persistently higher—averaging around 1½ percentage points higher— than the median of the rates you viewed as appropriate.	11	1
201506	557	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Second, the median of your federal funds rate prescriptions has varied more than the median of the Taylor-rule-implied rates.	11	1
201506	558	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	This suggests that the variables that appear on the right-hand side of the rule formula, including those longer-run levels for the unemployment rate and the real federal funds rate, do not encompass the factors that influence your forecasts—or at least they do not do so consistently.	11	1
201506	559	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Third, over the most recent three projections, the gap between the median Taylor-rule-implied rates and the median of your projections has widened somewhat.	11	1
201506	560	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Finally, before leaving this top panel of this exhibit, let me note the black dashed line.	11	1
201506	561	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	It shows the Taylor-rule-implied federal funds rate computed by taking the medians of your projected values of the rule’s right-hand-side variables to obtain a single federal funds rate reading per vintage—as opposed to taking the median of 17 projections of the federal funds rate, a method we followed in constructing the red line.	11	1
201506	562	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	As you can see, the policy prescriptions calculated using these two methods are quite similar; this is noteworthy because the calculation underlying the black dashed line can be performed with the information you currently provide to the public, whereas the calculation behind the red line requires knowledge of your individual projections.	11	1
201506	563	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	The bottom panel of the exhibit is devoted to this discrepancy between participants’ federal funds rate projections and your Taylor-rule-implied rates, with the individual residual values shown by the green open circles.	11	1
201506	564	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	There are some noteworthy outliers among these residuals, but the bulk of the values are reasonably well clustered.	11	1
201506	565	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	The two lines in the panel are medians, analogous to the red and black lines in the upper panel.	11	1
201506	566	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	In particular, the green solid line connects the median values of these residuals, while the black dashed line shows deviations of the median federal funds rate projections from the median Taylor-rule-implied rate by forecast.	11	1
201506	567	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Note the close correlation of these two lines—the black dashed line, especially—with the blue line in the top panel.	11	1
201506	568	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	This pattern highlights the fact that much of the time variation in your federal funds rate forecasts stems not from changes in the projected variables that appear on the right-hand side of the rule equation—unemployment and inflation—but rather from other, unspecified factors.	11	1
201506	569	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	How to interpret these residuals is an open question.	11	1
201506	570	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	We can infer that whatever explains them has been persistent and is expected to continue.	11	1
201506	571	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	One interpretation is that they represent your practicing “risk management” in the face of the effective lower bound problem and other asymmetric risks in the current economic environment; another is that they represent your perception of a temporary, if persistent, decline in the equilibrium real interest rate.	11	1
201506	572	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	Either interpretation could serve as justification for “keeping the target federal funds rate below levels the Committee views as normal in the longer run.”	11	1
201506	573	2015-06-17	meeting	TETLOW	That concludes my remarks.	11	1

201506	616	2015-06-17	meeting	FOLLETTE	Just as a little background to that, over the forecast period—2015 to 2017—we’ve got productivity growth, in terms of the trend, of around 1.6 percent, which is similar to the pre-1995 experience.	12	1

201506	724	2015-06-17	meeting	YELLEN	Seeing none, why don’t we begin our go-round on the economy.	13	1
201506	725	2015-06-17	meeting	YELLEN	We’ll start with President Williams.	13	1
201506	726	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	I won’t talk about r*.	13	1
201506	727	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201506	728	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	I view the very weak reading on first-quarter real GDP growth as mostly anomalous, with essentially no signal about the underlying pace of growth for the rest of this year or next year.	13	1
201506	729	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	It’s clear—and I think the Board analysis and the Tealbook did a very nice job on this—that a variety of transitory special factors were at work, although the precise quantification and when they will be offset in later quarters is still open to quite some debate.	13	1
201506	730	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	Like others, my staff has examined this issue and found that imperfect measurement of seasonal patterns played a large role in depressing reported Q1 numbers.	13	1
201506	731	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	Most of this residual seasonality appears to be caused by the BEA’s bottom-up methodology that seasonally adjusts the individual subcomponents of GDP but not the aggregate number directly.	13	1
201506	732	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	And the BEA uses this procedure to create the national income accounts.	13	1
201506	733	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	They are logically consistent at every level of disaggregation, and that makes perfect sense for their purposes, but for monetary policy purposes, it’s more useful to have the most accurate top-line measure of the broad economy.	13	1
201506	734	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	That is, it’s best to focus on eliminating seasonality in the aggregate GDP series and not worry so much about how the subcomponents should be adjusted.	13	1
201506	735	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	To provide such a top-line correction to the data, my staff conducted their own second- round seasonal adjustment of the published data on three measures of aggregate growth:  GDP; GDI; and the Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia’s GDPplus measure, which is a smoothed combination of GDP and GDI.	13	1
201506	736	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	After this correction, GDP is estimated to have grown about 1½ percent in Q1, GDI about 3 percent, and GDPplus about 2¾ percent.	13	1
201506	737	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	Based on these corrected numbers and relative to a trend growth rate of 2 percent or even lower, I am hard- pressed to describe the first-quarter growth really as weak or lackluster or disappointing.	13	1
201506	738	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	In addition, the second round of seasonal adjustment informs about when the residual seasonality that acts to understate Q1 will be balanced by overstated growth later this year.	13	1
201506	739	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	While the Tealbook assumes the unwinding of special factors will result in stronger Q2 growth, my staff’s estimates push most of the overstatement of growth into the third quarter.	13	1
201506	740	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	What does that mean?	13	1
201506	741	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	It means we shouldn’t be looking for a Q2 spike in growth.	13	1
201506	742	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	Instead, most of the seasonal effect will likely show up in an elevated Q3 growth rate.	13	1
201506	743	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, looking at the more recent data since Q1, recent spending indicators have generally been moving in the right direction and are pointing to, I think, a solid trajectory for growth and good momentum.	13	1
201506	744	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	We got some very important, positive news on the economy on Sunday night.	13	1

201506	755	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	All in all, with solid underlying momentum in fundamentals, I expect growth to average about 2¾ percent over the next several quarters or perhaps even higher, then slow to a more sustainable pace.	13	1
201506	756	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	I see consumer spending as a primary driver of growth, supported by elevated household wealth and solid income gains owing to rising employment and higher wages.	13	1
201506	757	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	In that regard, I am encouraged by the signs of a pickup in compensation growth, with private-sector ECI up about 2¾ percent over the past year.	13	1
201506	758	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	And employer costs for employee compensation are up about 5½ percent over the last year, suggesting solid growth and higher-paying jobs.	13	1
201506	759	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	This rise in income seems to have buoyed the spirits of households as well, and we saw the pickup or the rise in expected income growth reported recently in the Michigan survey.	13	1

201506	763	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	With regard to Asia, I was largely reassured during my recent trip to Singapore, then Hong Kong, then Beijing, where I was joined by Governor Powell.	13	1
201506	764	2015-06-17	meeting	WILLIAMS	Our discussions with Asian policymakers suggested that emerging market economies are acutely focused on a US monetary policy liftoff.	13	1
